عار محسوس نہیں کرتے کیونکہ یہ تو ہبہ ہے اس سے اولیاء کی ناک اونچی ہوگی نہ کہ بچی۔
The Book of Marriage. Commentary: 'With her deficiency, so it resembles kafa'a, unlike remission after specification because it does not cause shame.' Translation: When a woman marries and sets a mahr less than her mahr al-mithl, the guardians have the right to object according to Abu Hanifa, until the husband completes her mahr al-mithl or separates from her. The Sahibayn said they have no right to object. This formulation of the issue indicates, according to Imam Muhammad, a retraction from his earlier view on marriage without a guardian. The Sahibayn's proof: What is above ten dirhams is the woman's right; the one who forgoes his own right is not objected to, like after specification. Abu Hanifa's proof: Guardians boast of high mahr and feel shame from low mahr, so it resembles kafa'a. Unlike remission after specification, which does not cause shame. Explanation: An adult woman marries for less than mahr al-mithl: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, guardians have the right to object; the husband must complete the mahr or separate, otherwise the judge will separate after litigation. According to the Sahibayn, guardians have no right to object. The author of Hidayah states that this formulation indicates Imam Muhammad's retraction from his earlier view that marriage without a guardian is invalid. He now accepts it is valid. The text's case is marriage without a guardian, so this formulation is true evidence of Imam Muhammad's retraction. In the original issue, the Sahibayn's proof: Up to ten dirhams is the right of Sharia; above that is the woman's right. By reducing the mahr, she forgoes her own right, and one who forgoes his right is not objected to. Abu Hanifa's proof: Guardians take pride in high mahr for their women and feel shame from low mahr. So reduction in mahr resembles lack of kafa'a, where guardians have a right to object. As for remission after specification, guardians do not feel shame because it is a gift, which elevates them.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether guardians (awliya') have the right to object when an adult woman marries for less than her proper dower (mahr al-mithl). The ruling is presented as a statement of the matn, followed by the two positions: Abu Hanifa grants the right to object, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) deny it. The commentator explains that the formulation of the issue in the matn supports Imam Muhammad's later retraction of his earlier view that marriage without a guardian is invalid. The Sahibayn argue that the mahr above ten dirhams is purely the woman's right, so if she forgoes it, no one can object, similar to a remission after specifying the dower. Abu Hanifa counters that guardians feel shame from a low mahr, as it reflects on family honor, akin to kafa'a (compatibility). Therefore, deficiency in mahr gives them a right to object, unlike remission (ibra') which is a gift and brings no shame. The passage highlights the Hanafi emphasis on the guardian's role in protecting family dignity, even where the woman is adult and legally competent.

Key Terms
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The appropriate dower for a woman of similar status in her family and locality, used as a benchmark when no dower is specified or when it is deficient.
کفاعت (kafa'a): Compatibility between spouses in social status, lineage, etc.; here it is used analogously for mahr deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why do guardians have no right to object to deficient mahr?
A) Because the mahr above ten dirhams is the woman's exclusive right.
B) Because the woman is adult and can make her own decisions.
C) Because the marriage is invalid regardless of the mahr amount.
D) Because the guardian must consent to the marriage itself.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is divided on whether a guardian can object to a deficient mahr: Abu Hanifa allows it based on family honor, while the Sahibayn deny it based on the woman's ownership of the excess mahr.

The Father's Authority to Set Non-Standard Mahr for Minor Children — An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father's marriage of minor children with deficient/excessive mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the validity of a father's marriage of minor children with a deficient or excessive mahr, explain the proofs of each, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, father's close kinship alone validates the marriage; for Sahibayn, actual consideration is required.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key question: can a father (or grandfather) validly marry his minor children when the mahr is set significantly below or above the customary mahr (mahr al-mithl)? The matn presents Abu Hanifa's ruling of validity and notes the Sahibayn's restriction. Our exploration will unpack the legal reasoning and the practical difference between the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is the father marrying his minor daughter/son with a mahr that deviates significantly from mahr al-mithl. The dispute: Abu Hanifa says valid, Sahibayn say invalid if deviation is excessive.
Focus on the condition of 'consideration' (nazar) as the pivot.
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Sahibayn argue guardianship is conditioned by consideration. Excessive deviation shows lack of consideration, so the contract is batil, analogous to selling minor's property at a loss.
They analogize marriage to financial transactions.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues consideration is internal; we rely on its external evidence — closeness of kinship (qarabah). Father/grandfather are presumed to have consideration, so their actions are valid.
Marriage has higher objectives than mahr, unlike pure financial deals.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the marriage is binding from the start. Under Sahibayn, it is batil if deviation is excessive; thus the minor can repudiate upon majority.
This affects the finality of the marriage.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ باپ نے اپنی صغیرہ بیٹی کا نکاح کیا اور اس کا مہر، مہر مشکل سے کم مقرر کیا باپ نے اپنے صغیر بیٹے کا نکاح کیا اور اس کی بیوی کا مہر ، مہر مثل سے زائد مقرر کیا۔ اور یہ کی اور زیادتی معمولی نہیں بلکہ غبن فاحش کے قبیل سے ہے تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک صغیر اور صغیرہ دونوں پر یہ نکاح نافذ ہوگا۔ لیکن باپ دادا کے علاوہ نے اگر ایسا کیا تو نکاح درست نہیں ہوگا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک اگر کمی اور زیادتی غبن فاحش کے طور پر ہے تو جائز نہیں اور نین بیر کے طور پر ہے تو نکاح جائز ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ و معنى هذا الكلام سے صاحبین کے قول لا یجوز کی مراد بیان فرما رہے ہیں ۔ کیونکہ صاحبین کے قول لا یجوز کا ایک مطلب تو یہ ہو سکتا ہے کہ اصل نکاح تو درست ہے لیکن کمی اور زیادتی درست نہیں۔ اس کو مہر مثل کی طرف لوٹا دیا جائے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ صاحبین کے قول کی مراد یہ ہے کہ ان کے نزدیک نکاح ہی جائز نہیں ہے۔
صاحبین کی دلیل کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ولایت مقید ہے شفقت کی شرط کے ساتھ پس شفقت کے فوت ہونے سے ولایت ہی باقی نہیں رہے گی تو اب مطلب یہ ہوگا کہ باپ نے بغیر ولایت کے نکاح کیا ہے اور بغیر ولایت کے نکاح باطل ہوتا ہے لہذا یہ نکاح بھی باطل ہوگا اور رہی یہ بات کہ شفقت کیسے فوت ہو گئی سو عرض ہے کہ صغیرہ کے مہر مثل میں غیر معمولی کمی کرنا یا صغیر کی بیوی کے مہر مثل میں غیر معمولی زیادتی کرنا کون سی شفقت ہے اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ باپ نے صغیر یا صغیرہ کا مال فروخت کیا غین فاحش کے ساتھ کم قیمت پر یا ان کے لئے کچھ خریدا بہت زیادہ قیمت پر تو یہ بالا تفاق جائز نہیں لہذا نکاح بھی جائز نہ ہونا چاہئے اور یہی وجہ ہے کہ باپ دادا کے علاوہ نے اگر ایسا نکاح کیا تو بالاتفاق جائز نہیں لہذا باپ دادا کریں تب بھی جائز نہ ہونا چاہئے۔ امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ نظر اور شفقت امر باطن ہے اس پر حکم لگانا تو اس محال ہے۔ لہذا شفقت کی دلیل اور علامت پر حکم لگایا جائے گا۔ اور شفقت کی دلیل ہے قرب قرابت تو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ باپ اور دادا میں شفقت موجود ہے لہذا اس پر جواز نکاح کا حکم لگا دیا جائے گا اور باپ اور دادا کے علاوہ میں چونکہ دلیل نظر یعنی قرب موجود نہیں۔ اس لئے ان کے کئے ہوئے نکاح پر جواز کا حکم نہیں لگایا جائے
If the father marries his minor daughter and reduces from her mahr, or marries his minor son and increases in his wife's mahr, it is valid for them, and not valid for anyone other than the father and grandfather. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: Reduction and increase are not valid except within the range people ordinarily tolerate. The meaning of this statement is that the contract is not valid according to them because guardianship is conditioned by consideration (nazar); when it is lost, the contract becomes void. This is because reduction from mahr al-mithl is not consideration at all, as in sale. Therefore, no one other than them holds such power. For Abu Hanifa, the ruling is based on the evidence of consideration, which is closeness of kinship, and in marriage there are objectives that outweigh the mahr. However, in financial transactions, property is the objective, and we find the evidence of consideration absent in others. Explanation: The father marries his minor daughter with a mahr less than her mahr al-mithl, or his minor son with a mahr more than the wife's mahr al-mithl. If the reduction or increase is significant (ghaban fahish), according to Imam Abu Hanifa the marriage is binding on both minors, but if done by other than father/grandfather, it is not valid. According to the Sahibayn, if it is a significant loss, it is not valid; if within ordinary tolerance, it is valid. The author of Hidayah explains that the Sahibayn's statement 'not valid' means the contract itself is invalid, not just the reduction/increase. Their proof: Guardianship is conditioned by affection (shafaqa); when affection is absent, guardianship ceases. Reducing or increasing mahr al-mithl exceptionally is not affection; it is like selling property of minors at a loss, which is unanimously invalid. Hence, the contract is invalid. Abu Hanifa's proof: Affection and consideration are internal matters; so we rely on their evidence, which is closeness of kinship. Thus, father and grandfather are presumed to have affection, so their contract is valid; others lack this evidence.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the father's (and grandfather's) authority to set a non-standard mahr when marrying off his minor children. The matn states that such marriages are valid for the father and grandfather but not for other guardians, according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn restrict this to cases where the deviation is within ordinary tolerance (ghaban yasir). The commentator clarifies that the Sahibayn mean the contract itself is invalid if the deviation is excessive, because guardianship is conditional on consideration (nazar/ shafaqa). Lacking consideration, the contract is batil, analogous to selling a minor's property at a loss. Abu Hanifa argues that since consideration is internal, we take its external evidence (closeness of kinship) as a basis. The father and grandfather are presumed to have consideration, so their actions are valid. Others, lacking this evidence, cannot make such contracts. The dispute illustrates the Hanafi methodology of using external evidence (qaraba) to infer internal states, and the sahihayn's stricter view that the guardian's action must clearly benefit the minor.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar): Consideration or looking after the minor's interest; it is a condition for valid guardianship in financial and marriage matters.
غبن فاحش (ghaban fahish): Excessive loss or deception in value; here it means a significant deviation from mahr al-mithl that no ordinary person would accept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is a father's marriage of his minor daughter with a mahr significantly below mahr al-mithl invalid?
A) Because mahr is a pillar of marriage and must be specified.
B) Because the father lacks guardianship for such a deviation.
C) Because the minor daughter must give consent.
D) Because the marriage cannot be performed without witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa presumes consideration from kinship, while the Sahibayn require manifestation of consideration, leading to different rulings on the validity of non-standard mahr for minors.

Father's Authority to Reduce Mahr for Minor Children: Hanafi Dispute
Dispute on father's mahr authority over minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position and Sahibayn's position on father's authority to adjust mahr, explain the evidence (nazar) behind Abu Hanifa's view, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A father may reduce his child's mahr because close kinship proves consideration; wealth is not the sole objective in marriage.

This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a father's authority to reduce or increase the mahr of his minor children in marriage. The central question is whether the father may set the mahr below or above the mahr al-mithl (fair mahr). Abu Hanifa holds the contract valid based on natural consideration from close kinship, while the Sahibayn require the adjustment to be negligible, analogizing to sale where excessive loss invalidates the contract. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate legal analogies and appreciate the objectives of marriage in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
Recognize that the dispute centers on the validity of a father's action when he marries his minor child and sets the mahr different from the mahr al-mithl.
The father may also be the grandfather.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules the contract valid; the father may increase or decrease the mahr as he sees fit.
His evidence is close kinship (qaraba) which implies natural consideration (nazar).
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold that reducing the mahr excessively is not consideration and invalidates the contract, unless the reduction is insignificant.
They analogize to sale where a gross loss (ghubn fahish) renders the contract invalid.
4
Analyze the proofs
Compare the two arguments: Abu Hanifa relies on the presumption of nazar from kinship, noting that marriage has objectives beyond wealth. The Sahibayn prioritize the financial aspect and use qiyas with sale.
The commentary refutes the analogy because in marriage wealth is not the sole objective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If the reduction is excessive, Abu Hanifa says the contract is valid; the Sahibayn say it is invalid unless the reduction is negligible.
The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحط عن مهر المثل ليس من النظر في شيء كما فى البيع ولهذالم يملك ذلك غير هما ولابي حنيفة ان الحكم يدار على دليل النظر وهو قرب القرابة وفى النكاح مقاصد تربوا على المهراما المالية هي المقصودة في التصرف المالي والدليل عدمناه في حق غيرهما
Reduction from mahr al-mithl is not consideration in anything, as in sale, and therefore that is not permissible for other than them (father and grandfather). And Abu Hanifa: the ruling is based on the evidence of consideration, which is closeness of kinship. And in marriage, there are objectives that outweigh the mahr, whereas wealth is the objective in financial transactions. And the evidence we find absent in the case of others.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The issue: if a father marries his minor daughter and sets her mahr below mahr al-mithl, or marries his minor son and sets the wife's mahr above mahr al-mithl, is the contract valid? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes, because the evidence of consideration (nazar) is close kinship; the father's natural love ensures he acts in the child's best interest. In marriage, there are objectives beyond wealth (e.g., lineage, character), so adjusting mahr may serve a greater good. The Sahibayn argue that reducing mahr shows lack of consideration, similar to selling property below market value (ghubn fahish), and thus the contract is invalid unless the reduction is negligible. The commentary clarifies that the Sahibayn consider the contract itself invalid if the reduction is excessive. Abu Hanifa's response: the analogy to sale fails because in marriage wealth is not the sole objective.

Key Terms
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): Fair mahr: the amount women of similar status receive, absent explicit agreement.
نظر (nazar): Consideration/regard for the ward's best interest; evidence of proper guardianship.
قرابة (qaraba): Closeness of kinship; used as indicator of natural consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the basis for the father's authority to reduce the mahr of his minor daughter?
A) The father's absolute ownership of the child's property
B) Close kinship as evidence of consideration (nazar)
C) The daughter's consent implied by silence
D) The principle of istihsan over analogy

Key Takeaway
The father's close kinship is evidence of consideration, giving him broad authority in mahr matters; the Sahibayn caution against excessive reduction, but the operative opinion is Abu Hanifa's.

Marriage of a Minor to a Slave: Father's Authority Overriding Kafa'a
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father marrying minor to slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a father marrying his minor child to a slave, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A father can marry his minor child to a non-kufu spouse if a greater benefit outweighs the lack of compatibility.

This passage delves into a subtle but important disagreement within the Hanafi school regarding the guardian's power to contract a marriage for a minor. The father, as the primary guardian, has broad authority, but does that extend to marrying his free child to a slave when social compatibility (kafa'a) is absent? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, part ways on whether such a marriage is valid, revealing a deeper tension between the principle of maslaha (benefit) and the prevention of harm (darar). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to see how Hanafi legal reasoning balances competing considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Core Issue
The father marries his minor free child (daughter or son) to a slave (or slave woman). The lack of kafa'a (social compatibility) is the central issue: a free person marrying a slave is normally not considered compatible.
Kafa'a in the Hanafi school includes lineage, profession, and wealth. Marrying a slave generally violates this.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Its Basis
Abu Hanifa rules the marriage valid (jā'iz). His reasoning: the father may disregard the normal requirement of kafa'a for a superior benefit (maslaha tafūquhā). For example, the slave's excellent religious character might outweigh social status concerns.
The text uses 'لان الاعراض عن الكفاءة لمصلحة تفوقها' – turning away from compatibility due to an outweighing benefit.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Its Basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule the marriage invalid (lā yajūz). Their basis: marrying a free minor to a slave is manifest harm (darar zāhir) due to the clear lack of compatibility. They do not see a benefit that can override such harm.
The text uses 'وعندهما هو ضرر ظاهر لعدم الكفاءة' – according to them it is manifest harm due to lack of compatibility.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa: the marriage is valid from inception. Under the Sahibayn: the marriage is batil (void) unless perhaps the minor ratifies upon reaching majority? The text does not detail the remedy; it simply says 'it is not permissible'.
The practical difference: status of the marriage, rights of inheritance, paternity of children, etc.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Authority Weight
The text begins by stating 'فهو جائز' (it is valid), signaling that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The text quotes 'قال وهذا عند أبي حنيفة أيضا' – he said: this is according to Abu Hanifa as well, implying it is the preferred view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و من زوج ابنته وهى صغيرة عبدا او زوج ابنه وهو صغيرامة فهو جائز قال وهذا عند ابي حنيفة ايضا لان الاعراض عن الكفاءة لمصلحة تفوقها وعندهما هو ضرر ظاهر لعدم الكفاءة فلايجوز والله اعلم
And whoever marries his daughter while she is a minor to a slave, or marries his son while he is a minor to a slave woman, it is valid. He said: This is according to Abu Hanifa also, because disregarding compatibility (kafa'a) for a benefit that outweighs it. And according to them (Sahibayn), it is a manifest harm due to lack of compatibility, so it is not permissible. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the father's authority to marry his minor children to a slave or slave woman, which lacks the usual requirement of compatibility (kafa'a) in lineage or status. Imam Abu Hanifa holds the marriage valid because the father may perceive a greater benefit (maslaha), such as the slave's religious piety, that outweighs the lack of social compatibility. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it a manifest harm (darar zahir) to marry a free minor to a slave, and thus rule the contract invalid. The text notes 'also' (ايضا) indicating this is another case where Abu Hanifa's view differs from the Sahibayn, similar to the previous mahr issue.

Key Terms
كفاءة (kafa'a): Compatibility between spouses in lineage, religion, profession, etc.
مصلحة (maslaha): Benefit or interest that justifies overriding the norm of compatibility.
ضرر ظاهر (darar zāhir): Manifest harm; an obvious detriment that cannot be ignored.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for Imam Abu Hanifa allowing a father to marry his minor daughter to a slave?
A) Slaves are always considered compatible with free persons.
B) The father may have a benefit (maslaha) that outweighs the lack of compatibility.
C) The daughter's consent is not needed.
D) The father is obligated to find a spouse for his daughter.

Key Takeaway
A father's authority to marry his minor child includes the power to override the requirement of kafa'a if a clear benefit outweighs the lack of compatibility, according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Agent Marrying the Principal in Marriage: Hanafi Permissibility
Validity of agent marrying himself to principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on an agent marrying himself to the principal, explain the distinction from sale agency, and evaluate the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
An agent in marriage is a mere conveyor; the conflict of rights that voids sale does not apply.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle issue: can a man act as his female cousin's agent and marry her to himself? The Hanafi school permits it, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i forbid it. The key lies in understanding the nature of marriage agency versus sale agency. By examining the reasoning, you will see how the Hanafi school consistently applies the principle that the marriage agent does not acquire any right in the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The passage gives two examples: a paternal cousin marrying his cousin to himself, and a woman authorizing a man to marry her to himself. The common element is that the agent is also the groom.
2
State the Dispute
The Hanafi position is that it is permissible and valid. Zufar (within Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i say it is not permissible.
Zufar's view is within the Hanafi school but is not relied upon (marjuh).
3
Analyze the Opposing Proof
They analogize to sale: one person cannot be both granter and recipient of a contract because that creates a conflict of interest. Al-Shafi'i makes an exception for a guardian (wali) due to necessity, but not for a mere agent.
The sale analogy is the core of their argument.
4
Examine the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response distinguishes marriage agency from sale agency. In marriage, the agent is a mere conveyor (mu'abbir/safir) who does not acquire any right in the marriage. Rights do not revert to him. In sale, the agent is a direct party and rights revert to him, creating a conflict. The agent's statement 'zawwajtu' (I married) inherently covers both the proposal and acceptance, so no separate acceptance is needed.
The term 'mu'abbir' emphasizes that the agent is a conduit, not an owner of rights.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Hanafi, the marriage is valid; under Shafi'i, it is invalid unless the agent is also the guardian (wali). In the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, Zufar's view is not relied upon.
The practical difference: a valid marriage vs. an invalid one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الوكالة بالنكاح وغيرها ويجوز لابن العم ان يزوج بنت عمه من نفسه وقال زفر لا يجوز واذا اذنت المرأة للرجل ان يزوجها من نفسه فعقد بحضرة شاهدين جاز وقال زفر والشافعي لا يجوز لهما ان الواحد لا يتصوران يكون مملكا ومتملكا كمافى البيع الا ان الشافعي يقول فى الولى ضرورة لانه لايتولاه سواه ولا ضرورة في الوكيل ولنا ان الوكيل في النكاح معبر وسفير والتمانع في الحقوق دون التعبير ولاترجع الحقوق اليه بخلاف البيع لانه مباشر حتى رجعت الحقوق اليه واذا تولى طرفيه فقوله زوجت يتضمن الشطرين ولا يحتاج الى القبول
Section on agency in marriage and other matters. It is permissible for a paternal cousin to marry his cousin to himself. Zufar said: It is not permissible. And if a woman authorizes a man to marry her to himself, and he contracts in the presence of two witnesses, it is valid. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for him, because one person cannot be both the granter and the recipient of ownership, as in sale. However, al-Shafi'i says in the case of a guardian it is a necessity because no one else can act for her, but there is no necessity for an agent. Our proof: The agent in marriage is a conveyor and ambassador; the contradiction is in rights, not in conveyance. Rights do not return to him, unlike in sale because he is a direct party, so rights return to him. And when he undertakes both sides, his statement 'I married' includes both parts and does not require acceptance.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of an agent marrying himself to the principal in a marriage contract. The specific example: a paternal cousin acting as agent for his cousin (the principal) marries her to himself. The Hanafi position permits this, while Zufar (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i forbid it. The key distinction is in the nature of agency in marriage vs. sale. In sale, the agent is a direct party to whom rights and obligations revert; hence a person cannot be both seller and buyer. In marriage, the agent is merely a conveyor (mu'abbir) or ambassador (safir); he does not become owner of the right to marry. The conflict of interest (tamānu') relates to rights, not to mere expression. When the agent says 'I married you to myself,' the statement encompasses both the proposal (from the principal) and acceptance (from himself as groom), so no separate acceptance is needed. Al-Shafi'i makes an exception for a guardian (wali) due to necessity, but not for an agent, but the Hanafi response argues that the same logic applies.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakil): Agent: person authorized to act on behalf of another in a contract.
معبر (mu'abbir): Conveyer: one who transmits the offer and acceptance without acquiring rights.
سفير (safir): Ambassador/intermediary: similar to mu'abbir, emphasizing non-ownership of rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school permit an agent to marry himself to the principal in marriage?
A) Because marriage is a bilateral contract like sale
B) Because the agent is merely a conveyor, not a direct owner of rights
C) Because there is always a necessity
D) Because the principal can always revoke the agency

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits an agent to marry himself to the principal because the agent in marriage is a mere conveyor, and the conflict of rights that invalidates a sale does not apply.

Father Marrying Minor Children to Slaves: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of minor's marriage to a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifa's position on a father marrying his minor child to a slave (non-kuf'), explain the Sahibayn's opposing view, and evaluate their qiyas (analogy) to sale.

Core Idea
A father may marry his minor child to a non-kuf' for a greater benefit, distinguishing marriage from sale.

In this passage, we examine a classical intra-Hanafi dispute about the validity of a father's decision to marry his minor children to slaves. While both sides agree that the father has authority (wilayat al-ijbar), they differ on whether kafa'ah (social compatibility) can be waived for an overriding benefit. Imam Abu Hanifa allows it, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) object, drawing an analogy to sale where gross fraud invalidates the contract. We will work through their proofs and the key distinction that refutes their qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rulings
Note that Imam Abu Hanifa holds the marriage is valid (ja'iz), while the Sahibayn consider it invalid. The father's act is not in dispute; the validity of the contract is.
2
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's proof
He argues that kafa'ah can be disregarded when a benefit (maslahah) outweighs the harm of lacking compatibility. The father's judgment of benefit is given weight.
Maslahah here is a specific, overriding interest, not a general concept.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof and their qiyas
They analogize marriage to sale: in sale, gross fraud (ghaban fahish) invalidates the contract because wealth is the sole objective. Similarly, lack of kafa'ah in marriage is manifest harm.
The qiyas is based on the principle that manifest harm invalidates contracts.
4
Understand the Hanafi response (refutation of the qiyas)
The text states that the analogy is flawed (qiyas ma'a al-fariq) because in marriage, wealth is not the only objective; other purposes (e.g., piety, family ties) outweigh it. Thus, overlooking kafa'ah may be kindness, not harm.
This is a classic istihsan (juristic preference) reasoning: the ruling is based on istihsan, not strict qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical difference: if the marriage occurs, Abu Hanifa says it is valid and no annulment needed; Sahibayn require annulment (faskh). The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, as the text supports it.
In fatwa, the view of Abu Hanifa is preferred due to the strength of the distinction.

Source Text

اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چهارم
گا اور یہ کہنا کہ مہر میں کمی کر دینا کوئی شفقت نہیں۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ نکاح میں مہر ہی مطلوب نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اس کے علاوہ اور بھی بہت سے مقاصد ہیں جو مہر سے بڑھ کر ہیں۔ پس بسا اوقات داماد کے دوسرے کمالات کی وجہ سے مہر کی کمی کو گوارا کر لیا جاتا ہو اور یہ عدم شفقت نہیں بلکہ عین شفقت ہے۔ اور صاحبین کا نکاح کو عقد بیع پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے کیونکہ بیع میں مال ہی مقصود ہوتا ہے پس اس میں اگر غبن فاحش ہو گیا تو کوئی شفقت نہیں رہی اس کے برخلاف نکاح کہ اس میں صرف مال مطلوب نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اور دوسرے بھی مقاصد، جو مال سے بڑھ کر ہیں ۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
صغیرہ بیٹی کا نکاح غلام سے یا صغیرہ بیٹے کا نکاح باندی سے کرا دیا تو نکاح درست ہے

Explanation: The scenario is that a father married his minor daughter to a slave, or married his minor son to a slave-girl. There is a difference of opinion on this. According to Abu Hanifa, it is valid; according to the two companions, it is invalid. The gist of Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the father's marrying to a non-kuf' is due to some benefit that is more beneficial and superior to compatibility. So there is no harm in marrying without compatibility. The proof of the two companions is that marrying to a non-kuf' is manifest harm, so this marriage is invalid.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling concerns a father marrying his minor daughter to a slave or his minor son to a slave-girl. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such a marriage is valid (ja'iz) because the father, as a guardian, is permitted to disregard the condition of kafa'ah (social compatibility) when there is a greater benefit (maslahah) to be achieved. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, holding that it is invalid because the lack of compatibility constitutes manifest harm (darar zahir). The text notes that the Sahibayn analogize marriage to sale, but the Hanafi position refutes this by distinguishing the purposes of the two contracts: in sale, wealth is the sole objective, so gross fraud (ghaban fahish) eliminates kindness; in marriage, wealth is not the only objective, so a reduction in mahr or compatibility can be tolerated for other benefits. The commentary affirms that the father's authority allows such a marriage when it serves a greater benefit.

Key Terms
الكفاءة (al-kafa'ah): Social compatibility in marriage, including lineage, wealth, profession, etc., that is normally considered for suitability.
صغیر (saghir): Minor child who has not reached puberty, under the guardianship of the father.
مصلحت (maslahah): Benefit or interest that outweighs the harm of lacking kafa'ah, such as religious piety of the spouse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a father may waive kafa'ah for an overriding benefit, and such a marriage is valid, distinguishing marriage from purely wealth-based contracts like sale.

Self-Agency in Marriage: The Mu'abbir vs Mubashir Distinction
Two cases of self-agency in marriage with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits a guardian or agent to marry himself to his ward or principal, and identify the key distinction between marriage and sale that underpins this ruling.

Core Idea
In marriage, the agent is a mere expresser; in sale, he is a direct contractor—this distinction determines validity.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a critical dispute about self-agency in marriage. The Hanafi school permits a guardian or agent to marry himself to his ward or principal, while Imam Zufar and al-Shafi'i object. The key lies in understanding the agent's role: is he a direct party or a mere spokesperson? We will trace the proof and see how the Hanafi school uses this distinction to resolve the conflict.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two cases ruled upon: (1) a paternal cousin, as guardian, marries his minor ward to himself; (2) a woman appoints a man as agent to marry her to himself. According to Abu Hanifa, both are valid.
The passage explicitly says 'yajūz' (it is permissible).
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam al-Shafi'i both object. Zufar says both cases are invalid; al-Shafi'i permits only the guardian case due to necessity.
Al-Shafi'i's exception for the guardian is key—he accepts the logic for guardianship but not for agency.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponents argue by analogy to sale: one person cannot be both the giver (mumallik) and receiver (mutamallik) of ownership in the same contract. Since marriage transfers ownership of the woman's mahr? Actually, in marriage, ownership is of the wife's body (for companionship) and the groom pays mahr. But the analogy is to sale.
The core objection: al-wāḥid lā yataṣawwaru an yakūna mumallikan wa mutamallikan.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis distinguish between marriage and sale based on the agent's role. In marriage, the agent is a mu'abbir (expresser) or safir (mediator)—he merely conveys the words. Rights (like mahr and maintenance) do not revert to him but to the actual parties. Hence, there is no conflict in him representing both sides. In sale, the agent is a mubashir (direct contractor) and rights revert to him, so acting for both sides creates a conflict.
The key phrase: 'al-wakīlu fī al-nikāḥi mu'abber wa safīr'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a paternal cousin marries his minor cousin to himself, the marriage is valid in the Hanafi school but invalid according to Zufar. According to al-Shafi'i, it is valid only if no other guardian exists. For the agent case, Hanafis consider it valid, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i declare it invalid.
This difference has real implications for families where the only available guardian is also the prospective groom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الوكالة بالنكاح وغيرها
ترجمہ یہ فصل نکاح میں وکالت اور غیر وکالت کے حکم کے بیان میں ہے
ويجوز لابن العم ان يزوج بنت عمه من نفسه و قال زفر لا يجوز و اذا اذنت المرأة للرجل ان يزوجها من نفسه فعقد بحضرة شاهدين جاز و قال زفر و الشافعي لا يجوز لهما ان الواحد لا يتصور ان يكون مملكا و متملكا كما في البيع الا ان الشافعي يقول في الولي ضرورة لانه لا يتولاه سواه و لا ضرورة في الوكيل و لنا ان الوكيل في النكاح معبر و سفير و التمانع في الحقوق دون التعبير و لا ترجع الحقوق اليه بخلاف البيع لانه مباشر حتى رجعت الحقوق اليه و اذا تولى طرفيه فقوله زوجت يتضمن الشطرين و لا يحتاج الى القبول
ترجمہ اور جائز ہے چچا کے بیٹے کے لئے یہ کہ اپنا نکاح کرے اپنے چچا کی بیٹی سے اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ جائز نہیں اور جب اجازت دی عورت نے کسی مرد کو یہ کہ نکاح کرے اس سے اپنا۔ پس عقد کیا دو گواہوں کی موجودگی میں تو جائز ہے ۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی مملک اور متملک نہیں ہو سکتا ہے جیسا کہ عقد بیع میں مگر یہ کہ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ولی میں ضرورت ہے۔ اس لئے کہ اس کے سوا کوئی ولی نہیں اور وکیل میں کوئی ضرورت نہیں۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ وکیل نکاح میں معبر اور سفیر محض ہے اور منافات حقوق میں ہے نہ کہ تعبیر میں اور حقوق نکاح وکیل کی طرف نہیں لوٹتے ، بخلاف بیع کے اس لئے کہ وکیل عاقد ہے یہاں تک کہ حقوق بیع وکیل کی طرف لوٹیں گے اور جب نکاح کی دونوں طرفوں کا ولی ہے تو اس کا قول زوجت متضمن ہو گا دونوں جزوں کو اور نہیں محتاج ہو گا قبول کا۔
تشریح: یہ فصل نکاح کی وکالت اور غیر وکالت کے حکم میں ہے۔ غیر وکالت سے مراد نکاح فضولی اور نکاح ولی ہے۔ کیونکہ اس فصل میں احکام وکیل کے علاوہ فضولی اور ولی کے احکام بھی مذکور ہیں۔ اور وکالت تو درحقیقت ولایت ہی کی فرع ہے۔ کیونکہ جس طرح ولی کا تصرف نافذ ہوتا ہے مولی علیہ پر اسی طرح وکیل کا تصرف نافذ ہوگا مؤکل پر صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ چچا کے بیٹے نے ولی بن کر اپنا نکاح اپنے چچا کی بیٹی سے کیا حال یہ ہے کہ وہ لڑکی صغیرہ ہے اور اس کے علاوہ کوئی دوسرا ولی نہیں ہے مثلا اس نے کہا کہ گواہ رہو کہ میں نے اپنا نکاح کیا فلاں لڑکی سے جو فلاں بن فلاں کی بیٹی ہے ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے۔ اور امام شافعی بھی جواز کے قائل ہیں۔ لیکن امام زفر نا جائز کہتے ہیں۔ دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک عورت نے کسی مرد کو وکیل کیا کہ میرا نکاح اپنے ساتھ کرلو وکیل نے دو گواہوں کی موجودگی میں عقد نکاح کر لیا تو یہ صورت ہمارے نزدیک جائز ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک نا جائز ہے۔ امام زفر اور امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی ایک چیز کا ایک زمانہ میں مملک اور متملک نہیں بن سکتا ہے۔ یعنی مالک بنانے والا اور ملک حاصل کرنے والا ہو یہ نہیں ہو سکتا جیسا کہ بیع میں نہیں ہو سکتا ہے اور یہاں یہی خرابی ہے۔ کیونکہ چچا کا بیٹا اپنی طرف سے مالک بننے والا ہے اور لڑکی کی طرف سے ولی بن کر مالک بنانے والا ہے۔ لہذا مملک اور متملک دونوں ہوا۔ اور یہ نا جائز ہے اس لئے یہ عقد بھی نا جائز ہو گا۔ اسی طرح دوسرے مسئلہ میں مرد چونکہ ناک ہے تو مالک اپنے والا ہوا، اور چونکہ عورت کی طرف سے وکیل ہے اس لئے مالک بنانے والا بھی ہوا۔ لیکن امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ولی میں چونکہ ضرورت ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کے علاوہ کوئی دوسرا ولی نہیں اس لئے پہلی صورت کو جائز قرار دے دیا اور وکیل میں کوئی ضرورت نہیں کیونکہ اس کے علاوہ دوسرے کو وکیل بنایا جا سکتا تھا اس لئے یہ صورت نا جائز ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ نکاح میں وکیل محض تعبیر کرنے والا اور سفیر ہے ذمہ دار نہیں اور منافات حقوق میں ہے نہ کہ تعبیر میں ۔ حقوق میں منافات یہ کہ ایک ہی شخص مطالب اور مطالب دونوں ہو ۔ مملک اور متملک دونوں ہو ۔ مخاصم مخاصم دونوں ہو۔ لیکن تعبیر میں منافات نہیں کہ مالک بنانے کے الفاظ کہتا ہے عورت کی طرف سے۔ اور مالک بننے کے الفاظ کہتا ہے اپنی طرف سے۔ اس کے برخلاف بیع ہے۔ کیونکہ بیع میں وکیل عاقد ہوتا ہے۔ چنانچہ حقوق بیع وکیل ہی کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں مؤکل کی طرف نہیں ۔ اور جب یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ ایک آدمی طرفین کا متولی ہو سکتا ہے تو مرد کا قول زوجت ایجاب و قبول دونوں کو شامل ہوگا قبول کے لئے علیحدہ کلام کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں۔ اس لئے کہ جب ایک شخص دو کے قائم مقام ہو سکتا ہے تو اس کی ایک عبارت بھی دو عبارتوں کے قائم مقام ہوگی۔
Chapter on agency in marriage and other matters. Translation: This chapter is on the ruling of agency in marriage and non-agency. And it is permissible for a paternal cousin to marry his uncle's daughter to himself, and Zufar said it is not permissible. And if a woman permits a man to marry her to himself, then he contracts in the presence of two witnesses, it is permissible. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said it is not permissible for them, because one person cannot be both the one who gives ownership and the one who receives ownership, as in sale. However, al-Shafi'i says regarding a guardian, there is necessity because no one else can be his guardian, but there is no necessity regarding an agent. And for us (Hanafis), the agent in marriage is a mere expresser and mediator, and the conflict is in rights, not in expression, and rights do not revert to him, unlike sale because he is the direct contractor, so rights revert to him. And if he undertakes both sides, then his statement 'I married' encompasses both parts and does not need acceptance. Translation: And it is permissible for a paternal cousin to marry himself to his uncle's daughter, and Imam Zufar said it is not permissible. And when a woman permits a man to marry her to himself, then he contracts in the presence of two witnesses, it is permissible. Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i said it is not permissible. Their proof is that one person cannot be both the giver and receiver of ownership, as in the contract of sale, except that al-Shafi'i says regarding a guardian there is necessity because no one else can be his guardian, but there is no necessity regarding an agent. And our proof is that the agent in marriage is a mere expresser and mediator, and the conflict is in rights, not in expression, and the rights of marriage do not revert to the agent, unlike sale because the agent is the direct contractor, so the rights of sale revert to him. And when he is the guardian of both sides, his statement 'I married' includes both parts and does not need acceptance. Explanation: This chapter is on the ruling of agency in marriage and non-agency. By non-agency is meant marriage by a guardian and marriage by an unauthorized person (fuduli). Because in this chapter, besides the rulings of an agent, the rulings of a guardian and an unauthorized person are also mentioned. And agency is actually a branch of guardianship. Because just as the guardian's disposition is effective over the ward, similarly the agent's disposition will be effective over the principal. The scenario: A paternal cousin, acting as a guardian, married himself to his uncle's daughter while she is minor and there is no other guardian besides him. For example, he said: 'Bear witness that I have married myself to so-and-so girl, daughter of so-and-so.' According to us (Hanafis), it is permissible. Imam al-Shafi'i also holds permissibility. But Imam Zufar says it is not permissible. The second scenario: A woman appointed a man as agent to marry her to himself. The agent performed the marriage contract in the presence of two witnesses. This scenario is permissible according to us. According to Imam Zufar and Imam al-Shafi'i, it is not permissible. Their proof is that one person cannot be both the giver and receiver of ownership at the same time, as in sale. And this same problem exists here. Because the paternal cousin is, from his own side, the one receiving ownership, and from the girl's side, as guardian, he is the one giving ownership. So he becomes both giver and receiver. And this is not permissible, so the contract is also not permissible. Similarly, in the second scenario, the man, being the groom, is the receiver of ownership, and because he is the agent of the woman, he is also the giver of ownership. But Imam al-Shafi'i says that in the case of a guardian, there is necessity, because there is no other guardian besides him, so he deems the first scenario permissible. And in the case of an agent, there is no necessity because another person could have been appointed as agent, so he deems this scenario impermissible. Our proof is that in marriage, the agent is merely an expresser and mediator, not responsible, and the conflict is in rights, not in expression. Conflict in rights means that one person is both claimant and defendant, both giver and receiver of ownership, both opponent and opposed. But there is no conflict in expression: he utters the words of giving ownership on behalf of the woman, and he utters the words of receiving ownership on his own behalf. This is contrary to sale, because in sale the agent is a direct contractor. Consequently, the rights of sale revert to the agent, not to the principal. And when it is established that one person can be the guardian of both sides, then the man's statement 'I married' includes both offer and acceptance, and there is no need for a separate statement for acceptance. Because when one person can represent two, his one expression can stand for two expressions.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the chapter on agency in marriage, discussing two specific cases of self-agency. The first case: a paternal cousin, acting as guardian (wali) for his minor cousin (since no other guardian exists), marries her to himself. The second case: a woman appoints a man as her agent (wakil) to marry her to himself. The passage details the ikhtilaf: Hanafis (following Abu Hanifa) permit both, while Imam Zufar prohibits both, and Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits the second but permits the first due to necessity (the guardian has no alternative). The Hanafi proof distinguishes marriage from sale: in marriage, the agent is a mere expresser (mu'abbir/safir) and not a direct party to the contract, so there is no conflict of interest in rights. Rights in marriage (like mahr, maintenance) do not revert to the agent/guardian but to the actual parties. In contrast, in sale, the agent is a direct contractor (mubashir) and rights revert to him, so acting for both sides would be problematic. Consequently, in marriage, when one person acts for both sides, his single statement 'zawwajtu' (I married) suffices for both offer and acceptance.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; appointment of a person to act on behalf of another in a legal capacity, here specifically in marriage.
ولي (wali): Guardian, specifically the paternal cousin acting as guardian for a minor girl when no closer guardian exists.
معبر (mu'abbir): A mere expresser or spokesperson; the agent in marriage is a conduit for the contractual words without being a direct party.
مباشر (mubashir): Direct contractor; a person who directly undertakes a contract and to whom rights and obligations revert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it permissible for a woman's agent to marry her to himself, despite the apparent conflict of interest?
A) Because the agent is considered the same as the guardian, who has authority.
B) Because the agent is a direct contractor (mubashir) but the woman's permission overrides the conflict.
C) Because the agent is a mere expresser (mu'abbir) and rights do not revert to him, so no conflict exists.
D) Because the agent is allowed to marry the woman only if she is a minor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between mu'abbir (expresser) in marriage and mubashir (direct contractor) in sale determines the validity of self-agency: in marriage, the agent's role avoids the conflict of being both giver and receiver of ownership.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
The Marriage Contract Where the Groom Acts as Guardian or Agent
Inter-madhab dispute on marriage validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a marriage where the groom also acts as the bride's guardian or agent, compare it with the views of Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, and identify the Hanafi proof from istihsan.

Core Idea
In marriage, the agent is a messenger, not a party, so the objection of combining transferor and transferee fails.

This passage from Al-Hidayah deals with a nuanced issue: can one person simultaneously be the groom and the bride's guardian or agent? Both scenarios raise a logical objection – a person cannot be both the one transferring ownership (mumallik) and receiving it (mutamallik). However, the Hanafi school, guided by istihsan, distinguishes marriage from sale. Let us examine the precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: (1) a paternal cousin marries his minor cousin while acting as her guardian (no other guardian exists). (2) a woman appoints a man as her agent to marry her to himself. Both involve the same person acting on both sides.
In the first scenario, the man is both guardian (acting for the bride) and groom (acting for himself).
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its authority
The Hanafi school holds both marriages valid. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Zahir al-Riwayah). Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagrees.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah presents this as the mufta bihi view.
3
Understand the opposing argument
Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i argue that one person cannot be both transferor and transferee simultaneously, analogizing to sale. Imam Shafi'i makes an exception for the guardian case due to necessity (lack of other guardian) but not for agency.
The opponents use qiyas (analogy) with sale: a person cannot buy from himself or sell to himself.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof (istihsan)
The Hanafi response distinguishes marriage from sale. In marriage, the wakil is a mere messenger (safir) and not the contracting party. The rights of marriage do not attach to the agent. The contradiction (munafat) is in rights, not in mere expression. Since the agent does not acquire rights, the roles of mumallik and mutamallik do not conflict here.
In contrast, in sale the wakil is the actual contracting party; rights revert to him, creating a genuine conflict.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The disagreement leads to different rulings: Hanafis uphold both marriages as valid without need for necessity. Imam Zufar invalidates both; Imam Shafi'i validates only the guardian case due to necessity.
In practice, Hanafi judges and muftis will enforce the marriage if contracted per Hanafi rules.

مملک اور متملک دونوں ہو ۔ مخاصم مخاصم دونوں ہو۔ لیکن تعبیر میں منافات نہیں کہ مالک منانے کے الفاظ کہتا ہے عورت کی طرف سے۔ اور مالک بننے کے الفاظ کہتا ہے اپنی طرف ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف بیع ہے۔ کیونکہ بیع میں وکیل عاقد ہوتا ہے۔ چنانچہ حقوق بیع وکیل ہی کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں مؤکل کی طرف نہیں ۔ اور جب یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ ایک آدمی طرفین کا متولی ہوسکتا ہے تو مرد کا قول زوجت ایجاب و قبول دونوں کو شامل ہوگا قبول کیلئے علیحدہ کلام کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں۔ اسلئے کہ جب ایک شخص دو کے قائم مقام ہو سکتا ہے تو اس کی ایک عبارت بھی دو عبارتوں کے قائم مقام ہوگی۔
Translation: And it is permissible for the son of the paternal uncle to marry the daughter of his paternal uncle, and Imam Zufar said it is not permissible. And when a woman gives permission to a man to marry her, then he performs the contract in the presence of two witnesses, it is permissible. Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i said it is not permissible. Their evidence is that a man cannot be the one who transfers ownership and the one who takes ownership at the same time, like in a sale contract, except that Imam Shafi'i says that in the case of a guardian there is a necessity because there is no other guardian, but in the case of an agent there is no necessity. Our evidence is that the agent in marriage is merely an expressor and messenger, and the contradiction is in rights not in expression, and the rights of marriage do not revert to the agent, unlike sale because the agent is the contracting party such that the rights of sale revert to the agent. And when a person is the guardian of both sides of the marriage, his saying 'I have married' will encompass both parts and will not need acceptance. Explanatory chapter: The ruling of agency in marriage and non-agency. Non-agency means fuduli marriage and guardian marriage. Because in this chapter, the rulings of the agent as well as the fuduli and guardian are mentioned. And agency is really a branch of guardianship. Because just as the guardian's disposition is effective over the ward, so the agent's disposition will be effective over the principal. The case: The son of the paternal uncle acting as guardian marries himself to the daughter of his paternal uncle, while she is a minor and there is no other guardian. For example, he said, 'Be witnesses that I have married myself to such-and-such girl, daughter of so-and-so.' According to us, it is permissible. Imam Shafi'i also permits it, but Imam Zufar says it is not permissible. The second case: A woman appoints a man as her agent to marry her to himself. The agent performs the marriage contract in the presence of two witnesses. According to us, this is permissible. According to Imam Zufar and Imam Shafi'i, it is not permissible. Their evidence: A man cannot be the one who transfers ownership and the one who acquires ownership at the same time. That is, he cannot be the one who makes someone owner and the one who becomes owner, as is not possible in sale. Here the same defect exists: the son of the paternal uncle is becoming owner from his side and making owner as guardian from the girl's side, so he is both the transferor and the transferee. Similarly in the second case, the man is the groom (owner of himself) and also the agent (making owner). Imam Shafi'i says that in the guardian case there is necessity because there is no other guardian, so he permits it, but in the agency case there is no necessity because another agent could have been appointed, so it is not permissible. Our evidence: In marriage, the agent is merely an expressor and messenger, not a guarantor. The contradiction is in rights, not in expression. The contradiction in rights is that the same person is both claimant and respondent, transferor and transferee, litigant and opponent. But there is no contradiction in expression because he says the words of giving ownership from the woman's side and the words of taking ownership from his own side. This is unlike sale because in sale the agent is the contracting party, so the rights of sale revert to the agent, not the principal. And when it is established that one person can be the administrator of both sides, the man's statement 'zawwajtu' will encompass both offer and acceptance; there is no need for separate utterance for acceptance, because when one person can represent two, his single expression can represent two expressions.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute covering two legal scenarios: (1) a paternal cousin marrying his minor cousin while acting as her guardian (since no other guardian exists), and (2) a woman appointing a man as her agent to marry her to himself. The Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and followed by Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf (though not explicitly differentiated here), deems both marriages valid. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and Imam Shafi'i initially oppose based on the principle that one cannot simultaneously be the transferor (mumallik) and transferee (mutamallik) of ownership, analogous to sale. However, Imam Shafi'i makes an exception for the guardian case due to necessity (lack of alternative guardian) but retains the objection for agency. The Hanafi response distinguishes marriage from sale: in marriage, the agent is a mere messenger (safir) and the rights of marriage do not attach to him; the contradiction is in rights (which do not apply here because the agent is not the owner of the rights) not in the mere expression. Therefore, a single statement 'zawwajtu' suffices for both ijab and qabul, as one person can represent both parties. The passage also clarifies that agency is a branch of guardianship (wilayah) and the rulings apply similarly.

Key Terms
مملک (mumallik): the one who transfers ownership (e.g., the guardian giving the bride in marriage)
متملک (mutamallik): the one who takes ownership (e.g., the groom acquiring marital rights)
وکیل (wakil): an agent appointed to perform a marriage contract on behalf of another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi proof given in the passage, why is a marriage valid when a man acts as both the groom and the bride's agent?
A) Because the agent is considered the same as the guardian, and guardians have special authority.
B) Because the agent is merely a messenger, and the contradiction is in rights, not in expression.
C) Because the woman's permission creates necessity, overriding the objection.
D) Because the marriage is temporary until the woman confirms it, similar to a fuduli contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a marriage contract where the groom also acts as the bride's guardian or agent is valid because the agent is a mere messenger and the contradiction of simultaneous transferor/transferee applies only to rights, not to the act of expression.

Nikah — Marriage

The Ruling on Fuduli Marriage: Suspended or Void?
Ruling on suspended validity of slave marriage and fuduli marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on fuduli marriage, explain its proof, and compare it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A fuduli marriage is validly concluded but suspended pending consent—its ruling is deferred, not void.

This lesson addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the validity of contracts made by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The Hanafi school maintains that such contracts are not void from the outset but are validly concluded and suspended pending the consent of the relevant party. This has significant practical implications for marriages contracted without direct authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two specific cases in the passage
The passage discusses (a) marriage by a slave without the master's permission, and (b) marriage by a fuduli on behalf of someone else. Both are subject to the same ruling.
These are the illustrative examples given to establish the general principle.
2
Extract the Hanafi ruling and its proof
The Hanafi ruling is that the contract is validly concluded but suspended (mawquf). The proof cites the presence of rukn (offer and acceptance from a qualified person) and the absence of harm, along with an analogy to sale with option (khiyar).
The proof is found in the phrase: 'رُكْنُ التَّصَرُّفِ صَدَرَ مِنْ أَهْلِهِ مُضَافًا إِلَى مَحَلِّهِ وَلَا ضَرَرَ فِي انْعِقَادِهِ'
3
Extract the Shafi'i ruling and its proof
Imam Shafi'i holds that all fuduli actions are void (batil). His proof is that the contract is instituted for its ruling, and a fuduli cannot establish the ruling, so it is futile (laghw).
The Shafi'i position is presented as a direct contrast to the Hanafi view.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If consent is given later, Hanafi says the marriage becomes effective from the moment of consent; Shafi'i says it remains void and a new contract is required.
This difference impacts whether parties can rely on a single contract or need to renew it.

لہذا لغو ہوگا۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ رکن تصرف صادر ہوا ہے اس کے اہل سے منسوب ہے اس کے محل کی طرف اور کوئی ضرر نہیں عقد کے منعقد ہونے میں پس موقوف ہو کر منعقد ہوگا یہاں تک کہ اگر اسمیں مصلحت دیکھے تو ۔ کیونکہ یہ عقد موقوفاً منعقد ہوگا اگر مناسب سمجھے نافذ کر دے ورنہ رد کر دے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل کہ فضولی اثبات حکم پر قادر نہیں۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں حکم معدوم نہیں بلکہ اجازت تک کے لئے مؤخر ہو گیا اور حکم عقد عقد سے مؤخر ہو سکتا ہے جیسا کہ بیع بشرط الخیار میں اس لئے کہ اس بیج کا لزوم اور نفاذ شقوط خیار تک کے لئے مؤخر ہو گیا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
He said: The marriage of a male slave and a female slave without the permission of their master is suspended; if the master permits, it is valid; if he rejects, it is void. Similarly, if a man marries a woman without her consent, or a man marries another man without his consent, it is valid according to us. This is because every contract that issues from a fuduli (unauthorized person) and there is a person who can give consent (mujiz) is concluded suspended pending consent. Imam Shafi'i said: All actions of a fuduli are void because the contract is established for its ruling, and the fuduli is unable to establish the ruling, so it is futile. Our proof: The essential element (rukn) of the action issues from one qualified for it and is attributed to its place, and there is no harm in its conclusion; therefore it is concluded suspended until if he sees benefit in it, he executes it, and sometimes the ruling of the contract is deferred from the contract. Translation: The marriage of a male slave and a female slave without the master's permission is suspended; if the master validates it, it becomes permissible; if he rejects, it becomes void. Similarly, if a man marries a woman without her consent, or a man marries another man without his consent, it is permissible according to us. This is because every contract that issues from a fuduli and there is a mujiz for it, it is concluded suspended pending consent. Imam Shafi'i said: All actions of a fuduli are void because the contract is instituted for its ruling, and the fuduli is unable to establish the ruling, so it is futile. Our proof: The rukn of the action, i.e., offer and acceptance, issues from a qualified person (sane, adult) and is attributed to its locus (a woman not prohibited), and there is no harm in the contract being concluded; therefore it is concluded suspended until if he sees benefit, he executes it. And sometimes the ruling of a contract is deferred from the contract, as in a sale with an option (bay' bi-shart al-khiyar). Explanatory note: The issue is that the marriage of a slave and slave-girl without the master's permission is suspended; if the master permits, it becomes effective; if not, it becomes void. The second case: If a man marries someone else without his permission, or a woman without her permission, it is permissible according to us, and the effectiveness of the marriage is suspended pending permission. According to us, the principle is that any contract issued from a fuduli and in the council there is someone who can accept the offer (whether the acceptor is another fuduli, an agent, or the principal himself) – this marriage is concluded suspended pending permission. Imam Shafi'i's school is that all actions of a fuduli are void. His proof: The contract is instituted to establish its ruling, and the fuduli is unable to establish the ruling, so his speech is futile. Our proof: The rukn of the action (offer and acceptance) issues from a qualified person (sane, adult) and is attributed to the locus of the contract (a woman not from the prohibited categories), and there is no harm in concluding the contract because it is concluded suspended; if the concerned party sees benefit, he executes it, otherwise he rejects. The response to Imam Shafi'i's argument that the fuduli cannot establish the ruling is that here the ruling is not non-existent but merely deferred until permission, and the ruling of a contract can be deferred from the contract, as in a sale with a condition of option, because the binding effect and execution of that sale are deferred until the expiry of the option. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios: (1) marriage contracted by a slave or slave-girl without the master's permission, and (2) marriage contracted by a fuduli (unauthorized person) on behalf of another without consent. According to the Hanafi school, both contracts are validly concluded but suspended (mawquf) pending the consent of the person whose right is affected (the master for slaves, or the actual person for fuduli). If consent is given, the contract becomes effective; if rejected, it is void from the beginning. Imam Shafi'i holds that all actions of a fuduli are absolutely void because the contract is meant to establish legal effects, and the fuduli is incapable of doing so. The Hanafi response is that the essential elements (rukn) of the contract are present: offer and acceptance from a legally capable person (adult, sane) directed to a valid subject (a woman not prohibited). There is no harm in the contract being concluded in a suspended state, as the ruling can be deferred, analogous to a sale with an option (khiyar) where the binding effect is postponed until the option period ends. The text also states a general principle: any contract by a fuduli, where there is someone in the session who can accept the offer (whether another fuduli, agent, or principal), is concluded suspended pending consent.

Key Terms
فضولی (fuduli): a person who acts without authorization (command or permission) from the principal, whether in marriage or other contracts
موقوف (mawquf): suspended, i.e., the contract is validly concluded but its legal effects are pending the consent of the person whose right is affected
مجیز (mujiz): the person who gives consent (ijazah) to a suspended contract, thereby making it effective

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of a marriage contract performed by a fuduli (unauthorized person) before the principal gives consent?
A) The contract is void (batil) and cannot become valid even with later consent.
B) The contract is validly concluded but suspended (mawquf) pending consent; if consent is given, it becomes effective.
C) The contract is valid immediately because the fuduli has authority by default.
D) The contract is considered a nullity only if the principal rejects it before consummation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a marriage contract by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is validly concluded but suspended (mawquf) pending consent, unlike the Shafi'i view that it is void ab initio.

Fuduli Marriage Without Acceptor: Invalid According to the Two Imams
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fuduli marriage and presence of acceptor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fuduli marriage when no acceptor is present in the council and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A fuduli marriage without an acceptor in the council is invalid according to two imams, but valid upon approval according to Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines a specific case within the Hanafi school: a marriage contracted by a third party (fuduli) where no acceptance is made on behalf of the absent party during the council. The ruling depends on whether an acceptor (mujiz) is present. We will explore the positions of the founding imams and understand the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The matn presents two cases: (1) a man says 'I have married such woman' but no one accepts on her behalf; later she permits. (2) Another person in the same council says 'I have given her in marriage' and later she permits. Note the difference in presence of an acceptor.
Scenario 1 has no acceptor; scenario 2 has an acceptor (the second speaker).
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two imams), the first scenario is invalid (batil) because the contract lacks an integral (rukn) – both offer and acceptance must occur in the same council. The second scenario is valid but suspended (mawquf) pending approval.
The presence of the acceptor allows the marriage to be suspended; without it, the contract is void.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf holds that a single person can act as fuduli from both sides or as fuduli from one side and asil from the other. The marriage is valid but suspended on approval. He analogizes to khul', talaq, and 'itaq 'ala mal, where a single utterance creates a suspended contract.
His qiyas is based on unilateral dispositions that become binding upon approval.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The two imams distinguish marriage from those cases because marriage requires mutual consent (ijab wa qabul) in the same council, whereas khul' and talaq are unilateral acts. Thus, absence of an acceptor means the marriage never formed.
The condition for a suspended marriage is the presence of a mujiz in the council.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: if no acceptor is present, the marriage is batil, not even suspended.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position and is preferred by later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب النکاح

کسی عورت کیساتھ نکاح کرنے پر کسی کو گواہ بنایا ، اس عورت نے اجازت دیدی تو نکاح باطل ہے

اسی طرح کسی کو کہا کہ تم گواہ رہو میں نے فلاں عورت کو فلاں مرد سے بیاہ دیا کا حکم

ومن قال اشهدوا انى قد تزوجت فلانة فبلغها الخبر فاجازت فهو باطل و ان قال آخر اشهدوا انى زوجتها منه فبلغها الخبر فاجازت جاز و كذلك ان كانت المرأة هى التى قالت جميع ذلك وهذا عند ابي حنيفة ومحمد وقال ابويوسف اذا زوجت نفسها غائبا فبلغه فاجاز جازو حاصل هذا ان الواحد لايصلح فضوليا من الجانبين او فضوليا من جانب واصيلا من جانب عندهما خلافاله

ترجمہ اور جس نے کہا کہ گواہ رہو تم میں نے فلاں عورت سے نکاح کر لیا پھر اس عورت کو خبر پہنچی ۔ پس اگر جائزہ قرار دیدیا تو باطل ہے اور اگر (اسی مجلس میں ) دوسرے نے کہا کہ تم گواہ ہو کہ میں نے اس عورت کو اس فلاں مرد سے بیاہ دیا. پھر اس عورت کو خبر پہنچی اور اجازت دیدی تو نکاح جائز ہوگا اور ویسے ہی اگر یہ سب عورت نے کہا اور یہ سب امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک ہے اور ابو یوسف نے کہا کہ جب عورت نے اپنا نکاح مرد غائب سے کیا پھر اس مرد کو خبر ملی۔ اور نکاح جائز کر دیا تو جائز ہے۔ اور حاصل اس کا یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی جانبین سے فضولی بنے کی یا ایک جانب سے فضولی اور ایک جانب سے اصیل بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا ہے طرفین کے نزدیک ابو یوسف کا اس میں اختلاف ہے۔

تشریح ... صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دو آدمیوں کو گواہ بنا کر کہا کہ میں نے فلاں عورت سے نکاح کر لیا لیکن اس جنس میں عورت کی جانب سے کسی نے قبول نہیں کیا اس کے بعد عورت کو اس نکاح کی خبر ملی عورت نے اجازت دیدی تو طرفین کے نزدیک یہ نکاح باطل ہے۔ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک جائز ہے اور اگر اسی مجلس میں دوسرے نے کہدیا کہ تم گواہ رہو میں نے اس عورت کو اس مرد کے نکاح میں دیدیا یا یوں کہا کہ میں نے اس عورت کی طرف سے قبول کیا پھر اس عورت کو نکاح کی اطلاع ملی اور اس نے نکاح کو جائز قرار دیدیا تو یہ نکاح جائز ہوگا۔ ان دونوں مسئلوں میں فرق یہ ہے کہ پہلے مسئلہ میں مجیز نہیں لہذا نکاح باطل ہوگا اور موقوف نہیں ہوگا۔ اور دوسرے مسئلہ میں مجیز موجود ہے لہذا موقوف رہے گا۔ کیونکہ نکاح فضولی کے موقوفا منعقد ہونے کی شرط یہ ہے کہ مجلس عقد میں مجیز موجود ہو اور یہی حکم اس وقت ہے جبکہ یہ سارا کلام عورت نے کیا یعنی عورت نے کہا کہ تم گواہ رہو کہ میں نے اپنے آپکو فلاں ابن فلاں کے نکاح میں دیدیا۔ پس اگر مجلس میں مرد کی جانب سے کسی نے قبول نہ کیا اور مر کو خبر پہنچی اس نے اجازت دیدی تو طرفین کے نزدیک باطل اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک جائز ہے۔ اور اگر کسی نے مجلس ایجاب میں مرد غائب کی جانب سے قبول کر لیا پھر خبر ملنے پر مرد نے اجازت دیدی تو نکاح جائز ہوگا۔ حاصل کلام یہ ہے کہ ایک آدمی ایک جانب سے اصیل اور دوسری جانب سے فضولی یا دونوں جانب سے فضولی یا ایک جانب سے فضولی اور دوسری جانب سے ولی یا ایک جانب سے فضولی اور دوسری جانب سے وکیل بننے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے یا نہیں ۔ ابو یوسف کے نزد یک صلاحیت رکھتا ہے چنانچہ ان کے نزدیک نکاح منعقد ہو جائے گا البتہ اجازت پر موقوف رہے گا اور طرفین کے نزدیک صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا لہذا نکاح منعقد ہی نہیں ہوگا۔ ہمارے علماء کا اس پر اتفاق ہے کہ ایک آدمی ایک جانب سے وکیل اور دوسری جانب سے اصیل یا ایک جانب سے ولی اور دوسری جانب سے اصیل یا ایک جانب سے ولی اور دوسری جانب سے وکیل یادونوں جانب سے وکیل بن سکتا ہے اور رہا یہ کہ ایک آدمی کا دونوں جانب سے اصیل بننا سو یہ امر محال ہے۔
And whoever said: 'Bear witness that I have married such woman,' and the news reached her and she permitted, then it is invalid. And if another said: 'Bear witness that I have given her in marriage to him,' and the news reached her and she permitted, then it is valid. And similarly if the woman said all of that. And this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And Abu Yusuf said: When she married herself to an absent man and the news reached him and he permitted, it is valid. The summary is that one person is not fit to be a fuduli from both sides or a fuduli from one side and an asil from one side according to the two, while Abu Yusuf differs from them.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning marriages conducted by a third party (fuduli) without authority when the absent party later approves. The matn distinguishes two cases: (1) A man says 'I have married such woman' in the presence of witnesses, but no one accepts on behalf of the woman; later she permits. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn), this marriage is invalid (batil). (2) Another person in the same council says 'I have given her in marriage,' and later she permits; this marriage is valid (ja'iz). The same applies if the woman herself makes the statement. The commentary explains: in the first case there is no mujiz (acceptor) in the council, so the marriage is void and not even suspended (mawquf). In the second case there is a mujiz, so the marriage becomes suspended pending approval. The underlying principle is that a single person cannot act as fuduli on both sides or as fuduli on one side and asil on the other according to the two imams. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that one person can serve both roles, and the marriage becomes suspended and valid upon approval. The commentary further notes ijma' (consensus) that a single person can be an agent (wakil) on one side and asil (principal) on the other, or wakil on both sides, etc., but being asil on both sides is impossible.

Key Terms
فضولی (fuduli): A person who acts on behalf of another without prior authorization (wilayah or wikalah).
اصیل (asil): Principal party in a contract acting for himself.
مجیز (mujiz): The person who gives permission/approval (ijazah) for a suspended contract.
شطر العقد (shatr al-'aqd): One part of the contract (either offer or acceptance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a man says 'I have married such woman' without any acceptance from her side, and later she permits, the marriage is:
A) Valid immediately
B) Valid but suspended
C) Invalid (batil)
D) Valid only if witnesses were present

Key Takeaway
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, a fuduli marriage without an acceptor in the council is invalid, not merely suspended.

Nikah — Marriage

Marriage Between Fudulis: Consensus and Reasoning Dispute
Explanation of consensus and intra-Hanafi reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a marriage between two fudulis or a fuduli and an asil is valid but suspended, and distinguish the reasoning of the two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) from that of Abu Yusuf regarding a single fuduli acting on both sides.

Core Idea
Marriage by two fudulis is valid by consensus; the dispute is whether one person can be fuduli for both sides.

This passage addresses an important consensus within the Hanafi school: a marriage contract conducted between two fudulis (unauthorized agents) or between a fuduli and an asil (principal) is valid, though suspended (mawquf) pending the principals' approval. However, the three leading Hanafi imams differed on the reasoning behind this ruling, particularly on whether a single fuduli can act on behalf of both parties. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze how the Hanafi masters apply qiyas and distinguish types of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus ruling
First, note that the text states a marriage contract between two fudulis or between a fuduli and an asil is valid (ja'iz) by consensus (bil-ijma'). This is the foundational point.
The ruling itself is not disputed among the three Hanafi imams.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute is not about the validity of the contract but about the reasoning behind it. Specifically, can a single person act as fuduli from both sides? The two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say no; Abu Yusuf says yes, using analogy to khul', talaq, and 'itaq.
This dispute affects the case where one person says 'I have married' without a second party.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's qiyas
Abu Yusuf argues by qiyas on khul', talaq, and 'itaq: a husband's unilateral utterance becomes effective when the wife or slave accepts later. Similarly, a single fuduli's speech should create a suspended contract.
He sees a common element: the need for offer and acceptance, but the other party's acceptance can come later.
4
Understand the two imams' rebuttal
The two imams argue that what is present (the fuduli's statement) is only shatr al-'aqd (one part of the contract), analogous to a case where both parties are present. A part does not form a contract. In sale, an offer alone does not create a contract. Similarly, marriage requires mutual offer and acceptance in the council.
They distinguish khul' and talaq as unilateral dispositions (tasarruf yamīn) that become binding by oath, not by mutual consent.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the reasoning of the two imams is relied upon (mufta bihi). Thus, a marriage contract entered into by one person acting as fuduli from both sides is invalid; but if two separate fudulis (one for each side) or one fuduli and one asil contract, it is valid and suspended.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
